id='LC689'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC690'><span class="nx">Buffer</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">prototype</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">readUInt32LE</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="kd">function</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nx">offset</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nx">noAssert</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="kc">true</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nx">noAssert</span><span class="p">);</span></div><div class='line' id='LC696'><span class="p">};</span></div><div class='line' id='LC697'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC698'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC699'><span class="cm">/*</span></div><div class='line' id='LC700'><span class="cm"> * Signed integer types, yay team! A reminder on how two&#39;s complement actually</span></div><div class='line' id='LC701'><span class="cm"> * works. The first bit is the signed bit, i.e. tells us whether or not the</span></div><div class='line' id='LC702'><span class="cm"> * number should be positive or negative. If the two&#39;s complement value is</span></div><div class='line' id='LC703'><span class="cm"> * positive, then we&#39;re done, as it&#39;s equivalent to the unsigned representation.</span></div><div class='line' id='LC704'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC705'><span class="cm"> * Now if the number is positive, you&#39;re pretty much done, you can just leverage</span></div><div class='line' id='LC706'><span class="cm"> * the unsigned translations and return those. Unfortunately, negative numbers</span></div><div class='line' id='LC707'><span class="cm"> * aren&#39;t quite that straightforward.</span></div><div class='line' id='LC708'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC709'><span class="cm"> * At first glance, one might be inclined to use the traditional formula to</span></div><div class='line' id='LC710'><span class="cm"> * translate binary numbers between the positive and negative values in two&#39;s</span></div><div class='line' id='LC711'><span class="cm"> * complement. (Though it doesn&#39;t quite work for the most negative value)</span></div><div class='line' id='LC712'><span class="cm"> * Mainly:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC713'><span class="cm"> *  - invert all the bits</span></div><div class='line' id='LC714'><span class="cm"> *  - add one to the result</span></div><div class='line' id='LC715'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC716'><span class="cm"> * Of course, this doesn&#39;t quite work in Javascript. Take for example the value</span></div><div class='line' id='LC717'><span class="cm"> * of -128. This could be represented in 16 bits (big-endian) as 0xff80. But of</span></div><div class='line' id='LC718'><span class="cm"> * course, Javascript will do the following:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC719'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC720'><span class="cm"> * &gt; ~0xff80</span></div><div class='line' id='LC721'><span class="cm"> * -65409</span></div><div class='line' id='LC722'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC723'><span class="cm"> * Whoh there, Javascript, that&#39;s not quite right. But wait, according to</span></div><div class='line' id='LC724'><span class="cm"> * Javascript that&#39;s perfectly correct. When Javascript ends up seeing the</span></div><div class='line' id='LC725'><span class="cm"> * constant 0xff80, it has no notion that it is actually a signed number. It</span></div><div class='line' id='LC726'><span class="cm"> * assumes that we&#39;ve input the unsigned value 0xff80. Thus, when it does the</span></div><div class='line' id='LC727'><span class="cm"> * binary negation, it casts it into a signed value, (positive 0xff80). Then</span></div><div class='line' id='LC728'><span class="cm"> * when you perform binary negation on that, it turns it into a negative number.</span></div><div class='line' id='LC729'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC730'><span class="cm"> * Instead, we&#39;re going to have to use the following general formula, that works</span></div><div class='line' id='LC731'><span class="cm"> * in a rather Javascript friendly way. I&#39;m glad we don&#39;t support this kind of</span></div><div class='line' id='LC732'><span class="cm"> * weird numbering scheme in the kernel.</span></div><div class='line' id='LC733'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC734'><span class="cm"> * (BIT-MAX - (unsigned)val + 1) * -1</span></div><div class='line' id='LC735'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC736'><span class="cm"> * The astute observer, may think that this doesn&#39;t make sense for 8-bit numbers</span></div><div class='line' id='LC737'><span class="cm"> * (really it isn&#39;t necessary for them). However, when you get 16-bit numbers,</span></div><div class='line' id='LC738'><span class="cm"> * you do. Let&#39;s go back to our prior example and see how this will look:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC739'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC740'><span class="cm"> * (0xffff - 0xff80 + 1) * -1</span></div><div class='line' id='LC741'><span class="cm"> * (0x007f + 1) * -1</span></div><div class='line' id='LC742'><span class="cm"> * (0x0080) * -1</span></div><div class='line' id='LC743'><span class="cm"> */</span></div><div class='line' id='LC744'><span class="nx">Buffer</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">prototype</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">readInt8</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="kd">function</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nx">offset</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nx">noAssert</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="p">{</span></div><div class='line' id='LC745'>&nbsp;&nbsp;<span class="kd">var</span> <span class="nx">buffer</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="k">this</span><span class="p">;</span></div><div class='line' id='LC746'>&nbsp;&nbsp;<span class="kd">var</span> <span class="nx">neg</span><span class="p">;</span></div><div class='line' id='LC747'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC748'>&nbsp;&nbsp;<span class="k">if</span> <span class="p">(</span><span class="o">!</span><span class="nx">noAssert</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="p">{</span></div><div class='line' id='LC749'>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span class="nx">assert</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">ok</span><span class="p">(</span><span <span class="nx">offset</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="kc">true</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nx">noAssert</span><span class="p">);</span></div><div class='line' id='LC979'><span class="p">};</span></div><div class='line' id='LC980'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC981'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC982'><span class="cm">/*</span></div><div class='line' id='LC983'><span class="cm"> * We now move onto our friends in the signed number category. Unlike unsigned</span></div><div class='line' id='LC984'><span class="cm"> * numbers, we&#39;re going to have to worry a bit more about how we put values into</span></div><div class='line' id='LC985'><span class="cm"> * arrays. Since we are only worrying about signed 32-bit values, we&#39;re in</span></div><div class='line' id='LC986'><span class="cm"> * slightly better shape. Unfortunately, we really can&#39;t do our favorite binary</span></div><div class='line' id='LC987'><span class="cm"> * &amp; in this system. It really seems to do the wrong thing. For example:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC988'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC989'><span class="cm"> * &gt; -32 &amp; 0xff</span></div><div class='line' id='LC990'><span class="cm"> * 224</span></div><div class='line' id='LC991'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC992'><span class="cm"> * What&#39;s happening above is really: 0xe0 &amp; 0xff = 0xe0. However, the results of</span></div><div class='line' id='LC993'><span class="cm"> * this aren&#39;t treated as a signed number. Ultimately a bad thing.</span></div><div class='line' id='LC994'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC995'><span class="cm"> * What we&#39;re going to want to do is basically create the unsigned equivalent of</span></div><div class='line' id='LC996'><span class="cm"> * our representation and pass that off to the wuint* functions. To do that</span></div><div class='line' id='LC997'><span class="cm"> * we&#39;re going to do the following:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC998'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC999'><span class="cm"> *  - if the value is positive</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1000'><span class="cm"> *      we can pass it directly off to the equivalent wuint</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1001'><span class="cm"> *  - if the value is negative</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1002'><span class="cm"> *      we do the following computation:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1003'><span class="cm"> *         mb + val + 1, where</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1004'><span class="cm"> *         mb   is the maximum unsigned value in that byte size</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1005'><span class="cm"> *         val  is the Javascript negative integer</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1006'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1007'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1008'><span class="cm"> * As a concrete value, take -128. In signed 16 bits this would be 0xff80. If</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1009'><span class="cm"> * you do out the computations:</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1010'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1011'><span class="cm"> * 0xffff - 128 + 1</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1012'><span class="cm"> * 0xffff - 127</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1013'><span class="cm"> * 0xff80</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1014'><span class="cm"> *</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1015'><span class="cm"> * You can then encode this value as the signed version. This is really rather</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1016'><span class="cm"> * hacky, but it should work and get the job done which is our goal here.</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1017'><span class="cm"> */</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1018'><br/></div><div class='line' id='LC1019'><span class="cm">/*</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1020'><span class="cm"> * A series of checks to make sure we actually have a signed 32-bit number</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1021'><span class="cm"> */</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1022'><span class="kd">function</span> <span class="nx">verifsint</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nx">value</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="nx">max</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="o">!</span><span class="nx">noAssert</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="p">{</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1164'>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span class="nx">assert</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">ok</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nx">value</span> <span class="o">!==</span> <span class="kc">undefined</span> <span class="o">&amp;&amp;</span> <span class="nx">value</span> <span class="o">!==</span> <span class="kc">null</span><span class="p">,</span></div><div class='line' id='LC1165'>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span <span class="mi">7</span> <span class="o">&lt;</span> <span class="nx">buffer</span><span class="p">.</span><span class="nx">length</span><span class="p">,</span></div><div class='line'     <div id="markdown-help" class="instapaper_ignore readability-extra">
  <h2>Markdown Cheat Sheet</h2>

  <div class="cheatsheet-content">

  <div class="mod">
    <div class="col">
      <h3>Format Text</h3>
      <p>Headers</p>
      <pre>
# This is an &lt;h1&gt; tag
## This is an &lt;h2&gt; tag
###### This is an &lt;h6&gt; tag</pre>
     <p>Text styles</p>
     <pre>
*This text will be italic*
_This will also be italic_
**This text will be bold**
__This will also be bold__

*You **can** combine them*
</pre>
    </div>
    <div class="col">
      <h3>Lists</h3>
      <p>Unordered</p>
      <pre>
* Item 1
* Item 2
  * Item 2a
  * Item 2b</pre>
     <p>Ordered</p>
     <pre>
1. Item 1
2. Item 2
3. Item 3
   * Item 3a
   * Item 3b</pre>
    </div>
    <div class="col">
      <h3>Miscellaneous</h3>
      <p>Images</p>
      <pre>
![GitHub Logo](/images/logo.png)
Format: ![Alt Text](url)
</pre>
     <p>Links</p>
     <pre>
http://github.com - automatic!
[GitHub](http://github.com)</pre>
<p>Blockquotes</p>
     <pre>
As Kanye West said:

> We're living the future so
> the present is our past.
</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
  <div class="rule"></div>

  <h3>Code Examples in Markdown</h3>
  <div class="col">
      <p>Syntax highlighting with <a href="http://github.github.com/github-flavored-markdown/" title="GitHub Flavored Markdown" target="_blank">GFM</a></p>
      <pre>
```javascript
function fancyAlert(arg) {
  if(arg) {
    $.facebox({div:'#foo'})
  }
}
```</pre>
    </div>
    <div class="col">
      <p>Or, indent your code 4 spaces</p>
      <pre>
Here is a Python code example
without syntax highlighting:

    def foo:
      if not bar:
        return true</pre>
    </div>
    <div class="col">
      <p>Inline code for comments</p>
      <pre>
I think you should use an
`&lt;addr&gt;` element here instead.</pre>
    </div>
  </div>

  </div>
</div>
